        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">

           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/tʃ/: 'ch' in 'China'">
            tʃ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-LBGreenwich.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6d/En-uk-LBGreenwich.ogg" title="En-uk-LBGreenwich.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">
            dʒ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/tʃ/: 'ch' in 'China'">
            tʃ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">
            dʒ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) is an area of
       <a href="/wiki/South_London" title="South London">
        south east
       </a>
       London, England, located 5.5 miles (8.9 km) east-southeast of
       <a href="/wiki/Charing_Cross" title="Charing Cross">
        Charing Cross
       </a>
       . It is located within the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Greenwich" title="Royal Borough of Greenwich">
        Royal Borough of Greenwich
       </a>
       , to which it lends its name.
      </p>
      <p>
       Greenwich is notable for its
       <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history" title="Maritime history">
        maritime history
       </a>
       and for giving its name to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Meridian" title="Greenwich Meridian">
        Greenwich Meridian
       </a>
       (0° longitude) and
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">
        Greenwich Mean Time
       </a>
       . The town became the site of a royal palace, the
       <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Placentia" title="Palace of Placentia">
        Palace of Placentia
       </a>
       from the 15th century, and was the birthplace of many
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">
        Tudors
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">
        Henry VIII
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">
        Elizabeth I
       </a>
       . The palace fell into disrepair during the
       <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
        English Civil War
       </a>
       and was rebuilt as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Hospital_(London)" title="Greenwich Hospital (London)">
        Royal Naval Hospital for Sailors
       </a>
       by Sir
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">
        Christopher Wren
       </a>
       and his assistant
       <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hawksmoor" title="Nicholas Hawksmoor">
        Nicholas Hawksmoor
       </a>
       . These buildings became the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Naval_College" title="Old Royal Naval College">
        Royal Naval College
       </a>
       in 1873, and they remained an establishment for military education until 1998 when they passed into the hands of the Greenwich Foundation. The historic rooms within these buildings remain open to the public; other buildings are used by
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Greenwich" title="University of Greenwich">
        University of Greenwich
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Laban_Conservatoire_of_Music_and_Dance" title="Trinity Laban Conservatoire of Music and Dance">
        Trinity Laban Conservatoire of Music and Dance
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The town became a popular resort in the 18th century and many grand houses were built there, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Vanbrugh_Castle" title="Vanbrugh Castle">
        Vanbrugh Castle
       </a>
       (1717) established on Maze Hill, next to the park. From the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgian_period" title="Georgian period">
        Georgian period
       </a>
       estates of houses were constructed above the town centre. The maritime connections of Greenwich were celebrated in the 20th century, with the siting of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cutty_Sark" title="Cutty Sark">
         Cutty Sark
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gipsy_Moth_IV" title="Gipsy Moth IV">
         Gipsy Moth IV
        </a>
       </i>
       next to the river front, and the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
        National Maritime Museum
       </a>
       in the former buildings of the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_School" title="Royal Hospital School">
        Royal Hospital School
       </a>
       in 1934. Greenwich formed part of
       <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
        Kent
       </a>
       until 1889 when the
       <a href="/wiki/County_of_London" title="County of London">
        County of London
       </a>
       was created.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">
        Toponymy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Toponymy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The place-name 'Greenwich' is first attested in a Saxon charter of 918, where it appears as
       <i>
        Gronewic
       </i>
       . It is recorded as
       <i>
        Grenewic
       </i>
       in 964, and as
       <i>
        Grenawic
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">
        Anglo-Saxon Chronicle
       </a>
       for 1013. It is
       <i>
        Grenviz
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
        Domesday Book
       </a>
       of 1086, and
       <i>
        Grenewych
       </i>
       in the
       <i>
        Taxatio Ecclesiastica
       </i>
       of 1291. The name means 'green
       <i>
        wic
       </i>
       or settlement' (from the Latin 'vicus').
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The settlement later became known as
       <i>
        East Greenwich
       </i>
       to distinguish it from
       <i>
        West Greenwich
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Deptford Strond
       </i>
       , the part of
       <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">
        Deptford
       </a>
       adjacent to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thames" title="Thames">
        Thames
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parish_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Parish-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the use of
       <i>
        East Greenwich
       </i>
       to mean the whole of the town of Greenwich died out in the 19th century. However, Greenwich was divided into the registration subdistricts of
       <i>
        Greenwich East
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Greenwich West
       </i>
       from the beginning of
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_registration" title="Civil registration">
        civil registration
       </a>
       in 1837, the boundary running down what is now Greenwich Church Street and Crooms Hill, although more modern references to "
       <i>
        East
       </i>
       " and "
       <i>
        West
       </i>
       " Greenwich probably refer to the areas east and west of the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Naval_College" title="Old Royal Naval College">
        Royal Naval College
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
        National Maritime Museum
       </a>
       corresponding with the West Greenwich
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ward_(politics)" title="Ward (politics)">
        council ward
       </a>
       . An article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">
         The Times
        </a>
       </i>
       of 13 October 1967 stated:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        <i>
         East Greenwich
        </i>
        , gateway to the
        <a href="/wiki/Blackwall_Tunnel" title="Blackwall Tunnel">
         Blackwall Tunnel
        </a>
        , remains solidly
        <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">
         working class
        </a>
        , the manpower for one eighth of London's
        <a href="/wiki/Heavy_industry" title="Heavy industry">
         heavy industry
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         West Greenwich
        </i>
        is a hybrid: the spirit of Nelson, the
        <i>
         Cutty Sark
        </i>
        , the Maritime Museum, an industrial waterfront and a number of elegant houses, ripe for development.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-5">
          [5]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Manor_of_East_Greenwich">
        Manor of East Greenwich
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Manor of East Greenwich">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Colonial_charters_in_the_Thirteen_Colonies" title="Colonial charters in the Thirteen Colonies">
        Royal charters
       </a>
       granted to English colonists in North America,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       often used the name of the
       <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">
        manor
       </a>
       of East Greenwich for describing the tenure (from the Latin verb
       <i>
        teneo
       </i>
       , hold) as that of free
       <a href="/wiki/Socage" title="Socage">
        socage
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">
        New England
       </a>
       charters provided that the grantees should hold their lands "as of his Majesty's manor of East Greenwich." This was in relation to the principle of land tenure under English law, that the ruling monarch (king or queen) was paramount lord of all the soil in the
       <i>
        terra regis
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while all others held their lands, directly or indirectly, under the monarch. Land outside the physical boundaries of England, as in America, was treated as belonging constructively to one of the existing royal manors, and from Tudor times grants frequently used the name of the manor of East Greenwich,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but some 17c. grants named
       <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">
        the castle of Windsor
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Places in North America that have taken the name "East Greenwich" include a township in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Greenwich_Township,_New_Jersey" title="East Greenwich Township, New Jersey">
        Gloucester County, New Jersey
       </a>
       , a hamlet in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Greenwich,_New_York" title="East Greenwich, New York">
        Washington County, New York
       </a>
       , and a town in
       <a href="/wiki/East_Greenwich,_Rhode_Island" title="East Greenwich, Rhode Island">
        Kent County, Rhode Island
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_settlement">
        Early settlement
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early settlement">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">
        Tumuli
       </a>
       to the south-west of Flamsteed House,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Park" title="Greenwich Park">
        Greenwich Park
       </a>
       , are thought to be early
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
        Bronze Age
       </a>
       barrows re-used by the Saxons in the 6th century as burial grounds. To the east between the Vanbrugh and Maze Hill Gates is the site of a Roman villa or temple. A small area of red paving
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tesserae" title="Tesserae">
        tesserae
       </a>
       protected by railings marks the spot. It was excavated in 1902 and 300 coins were found dating from the emperors
       <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">
        Claudius
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">
        Honorius
       </a>
       to the 5th century. This was excavated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">
        Channel 4
       </a>
       television programme
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Time_Team" title="Time Team">
         Time Team
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1999, broadcast in 2000,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and further investigations were made by the same group in 2003.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_road" title="Roman road">
        Roman road
       </a>
       from London to
       <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">
        Dover
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">
        Watling Street
       </a>
       crossed the high ground to the south of Greenwich, through Blackheath. This followed the line of an earlier
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">
        Celtic
       </a>
       route from
       <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">
        Canterbury
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">
        St Albans
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As late as
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">
        Henry V
       </a>
       , Greenwich was only a fishing town, with a safe anchorage in the river.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parish_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Parish-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Viking">
        Viking
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Viking">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the reign of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethelred_the_Unready" title="Ethelred the Unready">
        Ethelred the Unready
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">
        Danish
       </a>
       fleet anchored in the River Thames off Greenwich for over three years, with the army being encamped on the hill above. From here they attacked
       <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
        Kent
       </a>
       and, in the year 1012, took the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">
        Canterbury
       </a>
       , making Archbishop
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphege" title="Alphege">
        Alphege
       </a>
       their prisoner for seven months in their camp at Greenwich, at that time within the county of Kent. They stoned him to death for his refusal to allow his ransom (3,000 pieces of silver) to be paid; and kept his body, until the blossoming of a stick that had been immersed in his blood. For this miracle his body was released to his followers, he achieved sainthood for his martyrdom and, in the 12th century, the parish church was dedicated to him. The present church on the site west of the town centre is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Alfege%27s_Church,_Greenwich" title="St Alfege's Church, Greenwich">
        St Alfege's Church
       </a>
       , designed by
       <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hawksmoor" title="Nicholas Hawksmoor">
        Nicholas Hawksmoor
       </a>
       in 1714 and completed in 1718. Some vestiges of the Danish camps may be traced in the names of Eastcombe and
       <a href="/wiki/Westcombe_Park" title="Westcombe Park">
        Westcombe
       </a>
       , on the borders of nearby
       <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">
        Blackheath
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norman">
        Norman
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Norman">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">
        Domesday Book
       </a>
       records the manor of
       <i>
        Grenviz
       </i>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_division)" title="Hundred (county division)">
        hundred
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        Grenviz
       </i>
       as held by Bishop
       <a href="/wiki/Odo_of_Bayeux" title="Odo of Bayeux">
        Odo of Bayeux
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       his lands were seized by the crown in 1082. The name of the hundred was changed to
       <a href="/wiki/Hundred_of_Blackheath,_Kent" title="Hundred of Blackheath, Kent">
        Blackheath
       </a>
       when the site of the hundred court was moved there in the 12th century. A royal palace, or hunting lodge, has existed here since before 1300, when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_I" title="Edward I">
        Edward I
       </a>
       is known to have made offerings at the chapel of the Virgin Mary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plantagenet">
        Plantagenet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Plantagenet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Subsequent monarchs were regular visitors, with
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">
        Henry IV
       </a>
       making his will here, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_V,_King_of_England" title="Henry V, King of England">
        Henry V
       </a>
       granting the manor (for life) to
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Beaufort,_Duke_of_Exeter" title="Thomas Beaufort, Duke of Exeter">
        Thomas Beaufort, Duke of Exeter
       </a>
       , who died at Greenwich in 1426. The palace was created by
       <a href="/wiki/Humphrey,_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Humphrey, Duke of Gloucester">
        Humphrey, Duke of Gloucester
       </a>
       , Henry V's half-brother and the regent to
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">
        Henry VI
       </a>
       in 1447; he enclosed the park and erected a tower on the hill now occupied by the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich" title="Royal Observatory, Greenwich">
        Royal Observatory
       </a>
       . It was renamed the
       <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Placentia" title="Palace of Placentia">
        Palace of Placentia
       </a>
       or Pleasaunce by
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">
        Henry VI
       </a>
       's consort
       <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Anjou" title="Margaret of Anjou">
        Margaret of Anjou
       </a>
       after Humphrey's death. The palace was completed and further enlarged by
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">
        Edward IV
       </a>
       , and in 1466 it was granted to his queen,
       <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Woodville" title="Elizabeth Woodville">
        Elizabeth
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ultimately it was because the palace and its grounds were a royal possession that it was chosen as the site for
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
        Charles II
       </a>
       's Royal Observatory, from which stemmed Greenwich's subsequent global role as originator of the modern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">
        Prime Meridian
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor">
        Tudor
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tudor">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The palace was the principal residence of
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">
        Henry VII
       </a>
       whose sons Henry (later
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">
        Henry VIII
       </a>
       ) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_Tudor,_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edmund Tudor, Duke of Somerset">
        Edmund Tudor
       </a>
       were born here, and baptised in St Alphege's. Henry favoured Greenwich over nearby
       <a href="/wiki/Eltham_Palace" title="Eltham Palace">
        Eltham Palace
       </a>
       , the former principal royal palace. He extended Greenwich Palace and it became his principal London seat until
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitehall_Palace" title="Whitehall Palace">
        Whitehall Palace
       </a>
       was built in the 1530s. Henry VIII married
       <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">
        Catherine of Aragon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Cleves" title="Anne of Cleves">
        Anne of Cleves
       </a>
       at Greenwich, and both
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">
        Mary
       </a>
       (18 February 1516) and
       <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">
        Elizabeth
       </a>
       (7 September 1533) were born at Greenwich. His son
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI" title="Edward VI">
        Edward VI
       </a>
       also died there at age 15.
      </p>
      <p>
       The palace of Placentia, in turn, became Elizabeth's favourite summer residence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_17-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Green-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both she and her sister Mary I used the palace extensively, and Elizabeth's Council planned the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">
        Spanish Armada
       </a>
       campaign there in 1588.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stuart">
        Stuart
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Stuart">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1148" data-file-width="1500" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg/220px-Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg/330px-Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg/440px-Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adriaen_van_Stalbemt_-_A_View_of_Greenwich.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Adriaen_van_Stalbemt" title="Adriaen van Stalbemt">
          Adriaen van Stalbemt
         </a>
         's
         <i>
          A View of Greenwich
         </i>
         , c. 1632.
         <a href="/wiki/Royal_Collection" title="Royal Collection">
          Royal Collection
         </a>
         , London.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_I" title="James I">
        James I
       </a>
       carried out the final remodelling work on Greenwich Palace, granting the manor to his wife
       <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Denmark" title="Anne of Denmark">
        Queen Anne of Denmark
       </a>
       . In 1616 Anne commissioned
       <a href="/wiki/Inigo_Jones" title="Inigo Jones">
        Inigo Jones
       </a>
       to design and build the surviving
       <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_House" title="Queen's House">
        Queen's House
       </a>
       as the final addition to the palace.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">
        Charles I
       </a>
       granted the manor to his wife
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Henrietta_Maria" title="Queen Henrietta Maria">
        Queen Henrietta Maria
       </a>
       , for whom Inigo Jones completed the Queen's House. During the
       <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
        English Civil War
       </a>
       , the palace was used as a biscuit factory and prisoner-of-war camp. Then, in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Interregnum" title="English Interregnum">
        Interregnum
       </a>
       , the palace and park were seized to become a 'mansion' for the
       <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">
        Lord Protector
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       By the time of the
       <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(1660)" title="Restoration (1660)">
        Restoration
       </a>
       , the Palace of Placentia had fallen into disuse and was pulled down. New buildings began to be established as a grand palace for
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
        Charles II
       </a>
       , but only the King Charles block was completed. Charles II also redesigned and replanted
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Park" title="Greenwich Park">
        Greenwich Park
       </a>
       and founded and built the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich" title="Royal Observatory, Greenwich">
        Royal Observatory
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Prince James (later King
       <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">
        James II &amp; VII
       </a>
       ), as
       <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_York" title="Duke of York">
        Duke of York
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Admiral" title="Lord Admiral">
        Lord Admiral
       </a>
       until 1673, was often at Greenwich with his brother Charles and, according to
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">
        Samuel Pepys
       </a>
       , he proposed the idea of creating a Royal Naval Hospital. This was eventually established at Greenwich by his daughter
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_II_of_England" title="Mary II of England">
        Mary II
       </a>
       , who in 1692–1693 commissioned
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">
        Christopher Wren
       </a>
       to design the Royal Hospital for Seamen (now the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Naval_College" title="Old Royal Naval College">
        Old Royal Naval College
       </a>
       ). The work was begun under her widower
       <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">
        William III
       </a>
       in 1696 and completed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawksmoor" title="Hawksmoor">
        Hawksmoor
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">
        Queen Anne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_George_of_Denmark" title="Prince George of Denmark">
        Prince George of Denmark
       </a>
       continued to patronise the project.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hanoverian">
        Hanoverian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hanoverian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">
        George I
       </a>
       landed at Greenwich from Hanover on his accession in 1714. His successor
       <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">
        George II
       </a>
       granted the Royal Hospital for Seamen the forfeited estates of the
       <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_rising_of_1715" title="Jacobite rising of 1715">
        Jacobite
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/James_Radclyffe,_3rd_Earl_of_Derwentwater" title="James Radclyffe, 3rd Earl of Derwentwater">
        Earl of Derwentwater
       </a>
       , which allowed the building to be completed by 1751.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1805,
       <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">
        George III
       </a>
       granted the
       <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_House" title="Queen's House">
        Queen's House
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Asylum" title="Royal Naval Asylum">
        Royal Naval Asylum
       </a>
       (an orphanage school), which amalgamated in 1821–1825 with the Greenwich Hospital School. Extended with the buildings that now house the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
        National Maritime Museum
       </a>
       , it was renamed the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_School" title="Royal Hospital School">
        Royal Hospital School
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
        Queen Victoria
       </a>
       in 1892.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_IV" title="George IV">
        George IV
       </a>
       donated nearly 40 paintings to the hospital in 1824, at a stroke creating a gallery in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Painted_Hall" title="Painted Hall">
        Painted Hall
       </a>
       . These now form the Greenwich Hospital Collection at the National Maritime Museum. Subsequently,
       <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">
        William IV
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_of_Saxe-Meiningen" title="Adelaide of Saxe-Meiningen">
        Queen Adelaide
       </a>
       were both regular donors and visitors to the gallery.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Victorian_and_Edwardian">
        Victorian and Edwardian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Victorian and Edwardian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
        Queen Victoria
       </a>
       rarely visited Greenwich but in 1845 her husband
       <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">
        Prince Albert
       </a>
       personally bought
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Admiral_Nelson" title="Admiral Nelson">
        Nelson's
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">
        Trafalgar
       </a>
       coat for the Naval Gallery.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1838 the
       <a href="/wiki/London_and_Greenwich_Railway" title="London and Greenwich Railway">
        London and Greenwich Railway
       </a>
       (L&amp;GR) completed the very first steam railway in London. It started at London Bridge and had its terminus at London Street (now Greenwich High Road). It was also the first to be built specifically for passengers, and the first ever elevated railway, having 878 arches over its almost four mile stretch. In 1853 the local Scottish Presbyterian community built a church close by. The church was extended twice in the 1860s during the ministry of the increasingly well known Dr Adolph Saphir, eventually accommodating a thousand worshippers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1864 opposite the railway terminus, theatrical entrepreneur
       <a href="/wiki/Sefton_Henry_Parry" title="Sefton Henry Parry">
        Sefton Parry
       </a>
       built the thousand seater
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Theatre" title="Greenwich Theatre">
        New Greenwich Theatre
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Morton_(theatre_manager)" title="William Morton (theatre manager)">
        William Morton
       </a>
       was one of its more successful managers. The theatre was demolished in 1937 to make way for a new Town Hall, now a listed building under new ownership and renamed Meridian House.
      </p>
      <p>
       Greenwich Station is at the northern apex of the Ashburnham Triangle, a residential estate developed by the Ashburnham family, mainly between 1830 and 1870, on land previously developed as market gardens. It is now a designated conservation area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The present Greenwich Theatre, further to the east, was constructed inside the shell of a Victorian music hall. Beginning life In 1855 as an annexe to the Rose and Crown, the music hall was rebuilt in 1871 by Charles Crowder and subsequently operated under many names.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_and_the_present">
        Modern and the present
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Modern and the present">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">
        George V
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">
        Queen Mary
       </a>
       both supported the creation of the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
        National Maritime Museum
       </a>
       , and Mary presented the museum with many items.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Prince Albert,
       <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_York" title="Duke of York">
        Duke of York
       </a>
       (later
       <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">
        George VI
       </a>
       ), laid the foundation stone of the new
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_School" title="Royal Hospital School">
        Royal Hospital School
       </a>
       when it moved out to
       <a href="/wiki/Holbrook,_Suffolk" title="Holbrook, Suffolk">
        Holbrook
       </a>
       , Suffolk. In 1937 his first public act as king (three weeks before coronation) was to open the National Maritime Museum in the buildings vacated by the school. The king was accompanied by his mother Queen Mary, his wife Queen Elizabeth (later
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">
        Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother
       </a>
       ) and the Princess Elizabeth (now
       <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">
        Elizabeth II
       </a>
       .)
      </p>
      <p>
       Princess Elizabeth and her consort Philip, Duke of Edinburgh (who was ennobled
       <a href="/wiki/Baron_Greenwich" title="Baron Greenwich">
        Baron Greenwich
       </a>
       on marriage in 1947) made their first public and official visit to Greenwich in 1948 to receive the Freedom of the Borough for Philip. In the same year, he became a trustee of the National Maritime Museum. Philip, now the
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">
        Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh
       </a>
       , was a trustee for 52 years until 2000, when he became its first
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patron" title="Patron">
        patron
       </a>
       . The Duke of Edinburgh has also been a patron of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cutty_Sark" title="Cutty Sark">
        Cutty Sark
       </a>
       (which was opened by the Queen in 1957) since 1952.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Silver Jubilee of Elizabeth II">
        Silver Jubilee
       </a>
       of 1977, the Queen embarked at Greenwich for the Jubilee River Pageant. In 1987, Her Majesty was aboard the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peninsular_and_Oriental_Steam_Navigation_Company" title="Peninsular and Oriental Steam Navigation Company">
        P&amp;O
       </a>
       ship Pacific Princess when it moored alongside the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Naval_College" title="Old Royal Naval College">
        Old Royal Naval College
       </a>
       for the company's 150th anniversary celebrations.
      </p>
      <p>
       To mark the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Diamond Jubilee of Elizabeth II">
        Diamond Jubilee of Elizabeth II
       </a>
       , it was announced on 5 January 2010 that on 3 February 2012 the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Greenwich" title="Royal Borough of Greenwich">
        London Borough of Greenwich
       </a>
       would become the fourth to have Royal Borough status, the others being the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Royal Borough of Kingston upon Thames">
        Royal Borough of Kingston upon Thames
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Kensington_and_Chelsea" title="Royal Borough of Kensington and Chelsea">
        Royal Borough of Kensington and Chelsea
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Windsor_and_Maidenhead" title="Royal Borough of Windsor and Maidenhead">
        Royal Borough of Windsor and Maidenhead
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The status was granted in recognition of the borough's historic links with the
       <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">
        Royal Family
       </a>
       , the location of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">
        Prime Meridian
       </a>
       and its being a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">
        UNESCO World Heritage Site
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-royal_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-royal-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">
        Governance
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Governance">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Greenwich" title="Royal Borough of Greenwich">
        Royal Borough of Greenwich
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1258" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg/220px-Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg/330px-Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg/440px-Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_Met._B_Ward_Map_1916.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A map showing the wards of Greenwich Metropolitan Borough as they appeared in 1916.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Greenwich is covered by the Greenwich West and Peninsula wards of the London Borough of Greenwich, which was formed in 1965 by merging the former
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Greenwich" title="Metropolitan Borough of Greenwich">
        Metropolitan Borough of Greenwich
       </a>
       with that part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Woolwich" title="Metropolitan Borough of Woolwich">
        Metropolitan Borough of Woolwich
       </a>
       which lay south of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Thames" title="The Thames">
        The Thames
       </a>
       . Along with
       <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">
        Blackheath
       </a>
       Westcombe,
       <a href="/wiki/Charlton,_London" title="Charlton, London">
        Charlton
       </a>
       , Glyndon,
       <a href="/wiki/Woolwich" title="Woolwich">
        Woolwich
       </a>
       Riverside, and
       <a href="/wiki/Woolwich_Common" title="Woolwich Common">
        Woolwich Common
       </a>
       , it elects a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" title="Member of Parliament">
        Member of Parliament
       </a>
       (MP) for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_and_Woolwich" title="Greenwich and Woolwich">
        Greenwich and Woolwich
       </a>
       ; currently the MP is
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Pennycook" title="Matthew Pennycook">
        Matthew Pennycook
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">
        Topography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Topography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The town of Greenwich is built on a broad platform to the south of the outside of a broad meander in the River Thames, with a safe deep water anchorage lying in the river. To the south, the land rises steeply, 100 feet (30 m) through Greenwich Park to the town of
       <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">
        Blackheath
       </a>
       . The higher areas consist of a sedimentary layer of gravelly soils, known as the
       <i>
        Blackheath Beds
       </i>
       , that spread through much of the south-east over a chalk outcrop—with sands, loam and seams of clay at the lower levels by the river.
      </p>
      <p>
       Greenwich is bordered by
       <a href="/wiki/River_Ravensbourne" title="River Ravensbourne">
        Deptford Creek
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">
        Deptford
       </a>
       to the west; the former industrial centre of the
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Peninsula" title="Greenwich Peninsula">
        Greenwich Peninsula
       </a>
       and the residential area of
       <a href="/wiki/Westcombe_Park" title="Westcombe Park">
        Westcombe Park
       </a>
       to the east; the
       <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">
        River Thames
       </a>
       to the north; and the
       <a href="/wiki/A2_road_(Great_Britain)" title="A2 road (Great Britain)">
        A2
       </a>
       and Blackheath to the south.
      </p>
